The Bernoulli principles you're on about apply to closed pipes, applying them to an open system like this is way more complex.   Apart from anything else, the wave can and will get higher in the narrow part of the channel, which is another way of storing energy that the fluid in the pipe does not have. Thinking of things in 2D, it effectively makes it compressible.  Also, a tsunami wave is very different from a consistently flowing fluid in a closed pipe. To be simplistic, there is nothing behind the wave pushing it forwards, nor is there anything in front of it pulling it on, as there is with a fluid flowing in a pipe. The only thing that keeps it going is its momentum. A tsunami near shore behaves as q breaking wave, in other words a mass of water that continues on just because nothing has stopped it yet. You would be better thinking of it as a mass of bowling balls than as a fluid in a pipe.

I think the main reason it didn't work as well as a real Tesla-Valve would, isn't because it was symmetrical. It probably was because a Valve is constructed in such a way that it blocks a continuous flow of fluids and a Tsunami is one "wall" of water. You can see it in the construction:  When the water that flows back reaches the Tsunamis main wave, it's already further ahead and isn't really affected. The Valve with a continuous water flow would start to block the water that would come afterwards.  Maybe something to consider...

It always confused me that "valves" are devices that let you turn a fluid flow on/off, but _also_ "valves" are devices that prevent backflow.  I know that when a sealed pipe increases or decreases in diameter that changes the linear speed to conserve the volumetric flow rate, but does that even apply to open channels? The water getting deeper or shallower handily accounts for the same volumetric flow in the same cross-sectional area to have the same linear speed.  Also I'm a bit sceptical that the "tesla valve" is actually having any real effect on this, by the time the water is flowing backwards out of the baffles, the main wavefront has already passed. I suspect that simply having a bunch of piers sticking out into the flow _without_ looping backwards would have the same effect.

I think you're oversimplifying when you say wider = slower and that the game is not simulating this correctly.  Just think of a wide channel and add a wall parallel to the flow, but nearer to one side.  Ignoring the friction at the edges, the wall does nothing and it clearly will not affect the speed on either side. The narrow and wider parts will flow at the same speed.  It's only when you restrict a flow that it will speed up, but then if it widens again after the restriction, it also slows back down to maintain the constant mass flow.  Further more, here you're open from above, so the water can also pile up *instead* of speeding up, again maintaining the constant mass flow.

The description of the speed of the wave in smaller spaces is completely wrong.  What you were describing is true for pipes where there is nowhere for the water to go, but not true for a wave where there can still be upward displacement.  Instead wave speed is governed by the shallow water wave equations where you can show that wave speed is related to water depth.  i.e. the shallower the water the slower it travels.  This is why waves traveling at an angle to a shoreline will still refract toward the shore and often end up breaking nearly straight on.
Tesla valves are NOT symmetrical.

the game seems to use the shallow water equations which have a lot of limitations because it's height-based, one of them is that it doesn't follow the Bernoulli principle, the only pressure taken into account is the hydrostatic pressure so things like the negative gradient of the pressure of the Navier-stokes equations also are missing, viscous forces and shear forces are also ignored and velocity is conserved(as long as (âˆ‚h/âˆ‚x, âˆ‚h/âˆ‚y)=0 âˆ€x,y). the SWE is great for large masses of water like seas oceans, and lakes but not great for tesla valves  because velocity is purely dependent on (âˆ‚h/âˆ‚x, âˆ‚h/âˆ‚y)

While the speed of water flow will change if it is going through a nozzle or diffuser, the speed of the waveform will not.  This is because the water can simply go up, thus increasing the height of the wave.  If your channels were contained (had a lid or roof), then the water speed would change according to the cross sectional area of the channel.  But since there is no upper bound, the water just changes in depth instead.

Tesla Patent 1,329,559 "Valvular Conduit" means that the noun is conduit while the adjective is valvular. Proper specification would be "Tesla Conduit." The patent specifies that the device employs both expansion and contraction together with redirection to destructively interfere with the propagation of wave impulses in one direction. Key attributes include at least 10 degree alternating deviations in the main channel flow path which direct the main flow into a bucket thrust reverser on the opposite side of the channel.  This necessitates that the repeating walls are offset along the channel by one full stage length. The video simulation shows them opposite each other. Properly constructed the Tesla anti-tsunami channel would not allow a view of the city from the beach. Additionally, the Tesla conduit has a top plate which would prevent any overtopping.  IMO Tesla designed the conduit for fluid flow in opposite directions. It's not clear why simple flood walls would not suffice. If the intent was to limit tsunami penetration up a river, then that would make sense.

I don't think the maker is right that the speed should change because this "tesla valve" is not capped in the 3rd dimension (height). The reason the 2 d tesla valves increase speed in narrower channels  is because there is no third dimension for the water to stack up. 2d dynamics do not = 3d dynamics . The reason the 2d diagrams work is because each layer stacking in the 3rd dimension is assumed to be exactly equal to the other layers. The fact that there are height differences in a tsunami wave invalidates the 2d treatment.
Except... this is not a Tesla valve. At least do some basic research before making a video. You sure you are a real engineer?
For incompressible fluids velocity is proportional to cross sectional area. The wave doesn't go faster bc it's not constrained in the vertical dimension, so it deforms and gets taller and the cross sectional area stays the same. You'd need a pipe or something for the cross sectional area to get smaller

..except that this thing, while being successfully penis-shaped, is NOT a Tesla valve.  A Tesla valve doesn't have a straight unobstructed flow channel down the center.
In order to wrok, you need to make each step asymmetric. The one that takes the straight path must expand so that it travels more slowly, while the one that takes the bendy path must contract so it speeds up and makes it to the crossing point at roughly the same time

I don't think your statement "the wider the water, the slower it is" is not necessarily true. If a wide channel is funnelled down to a narrow channel then I think it would be true, but in this case the effective depth and wave amplitude would increase. It is also true that to get the same amount of fluid through a narrow channel compared to a wide one, flow must be faster. Of course all this assumes constant depth and depths differences will change the flow speed. If your statement was true, then the wave speed over the sea would be much slower than down your channel.

One thing is that I don't think the intuition for fluid in a pipe (with no air) is applicable to fluid with a free surface. On the simplest level, continuity equations don't work the same because height can change freely, so you don't have the same flow vs cross-section relation.

I also took some Hydraulics classes in college.  It's the Conservation of Matter equation that typically yields the increase in a fluid's velocity as it goes through an orifice. Namely, Q=v*A, where Q is the flow, v is the velocity of the fluid, and A is the cross section of fluid at any point of the flow line. If you have fluid flowing through a rigid container, like a pipe, shrinking the diameter will shrink the A, forcing an increase in v to preserve the same Q. However, in this example, A=w*h, where w is the width of the channel, and h is the height of the fluid. When you shrink or increase the width of the channel, you're not changing A, you're changing w. A proportional change in h (the height of the wave) leaves A the same, which also leaves v and Q the same, leading to the wave through the channel maintaining the same speed as wave in the parallel river.

Fluid mechanics is somewhat analagous to electricity. Although not a perfect analog. Its similar enough that expertise in one area can intersect with the other. It just requires a bit of translation.  For example. A narrow channel is analogous to a resistor. A capacitor is like some kind of pressure buffer.... elastic in its  effect... similar to a balloon. Something that can store pressure and release it when the pressure drops. But its weird to explain. Transistors are like valves which can open and close. (Although tesla would not have known about transistors.) The same concepts typically apply to both fields. Etc etc.

Sooooo.... awesome demo, and it totally works, but this isn't actually a Tesla flute. It's shaped like one, but Tesla flutes work by redirecting pressure back at itself. If you send two waves at each other, they'll pass through each other with minimal interference. This works because it spreads out the force of the wave across a wider area. Each of your barriers will spread the force of the wave out by roughly half, and the last one reduces it to a third.  Fun video, though.

No idea why this showed up in my feed today.  Not going to pretend to be a civil engineer nor any kind of expert in tsunamis, but I think your starting assumption is wrong.  If you watch the tsunami videos, it really does strike like a *tidal* wave.  I mean, "tidal wave" is actually a good name for it.  The water level suddenly goes up, but it doesn't go away, and then it goes up again, and doesn't go away.  It just pumps and pumps until it floods over everything.  It's just not a huge wave in a big wall of water that crashes in.  A system designed to dissipate energy isn't going to work.  You need a valve that actually shuts closed and stays that way until it's over.  Think of it this way... the first warning sign for a tidal wave coming in is that the water level in the bays goes *down* and it stays down long enough for curious people to wander down and get in trouble.  Long enough that people are warned not to go near the water when this happens.  Well, when the water comes in, it comes in for just as long, longer if it spills over into areas that don't naturally drain well.

The funnel works because it's not a river, it's a tsunami. In a river, you've forced the same amount of water into a narrower passage making it incredibly fast and dangerous because it's still got the entire river behind it pushing it forwards. A tsunami is not at sea level so the funnel introduces so much wave interference that much of the wave actually dissipates under it's own force. And because it's just a wave (essentially) it hasn't got a whole waterway behind it continuously providing force and pushing it forwards.

This is very similar to blood vein (the ones that return blood to the heart).  Inside each vein are elastic pieces of tissue that will close when gravity pulls on the blood and will open when the heart pumps the blood up the vein.  The elastic tissue is inverted (compared to your design) in veins because they need to catch the fluid (blood) instead of repel it.  While your "Tesla valve" gates don't close, they definitely are representative of the same design (although inverted).

10:30 really gross error there. the flow speed slows down when a fixed volume per time of fluid is passing through widening cross sectional area. In this case, however, the cross section of the water doesn't necessarily increase because it gets lower while also being wider, and the flow speed could actually even be quicker depending on friction parameters because the water gained energy by falling. A similarly opposite is true for the narrowing case.

to anyone not understanding: a tesla valve works by dividing the wave(or water flow) and using it against itself. the baffles turn some of the flow to collide with the opposite flow. much like noise canceling headphones work (soundwaves collide with each other cancelling each other out)

Yes, the giant penis is absolutely a structural thing. Super important for integrity.
It doesn't speed up because you left it somewhere to go. It gets taller because the narrowness requires it to conserve energy by storing it in potential energy. If you had something to restrict the height that it could store it at, that would result in an increase in pressure and then you would have your increase in speed.... That's also not how Tesla valves work. Tesla valves work in principle by wave cancellation. They essentially reflect the wave onto itself which reduces its strength. They don't slow the wave down... You keep saying that the simulation is wrong and that that wouldn't happen in real life... But the physics simulation actually looks extremely accurate to me. Wave speed has nothing to do with the width of the channel. You're confusing Bernoulli's PRINCIPLE with Boyle's law.  However, it is the Lagrangian that determines wave speed and that is dictated by density, and temperature. Since both of those are constant in your simulation, the speed of the wave is also constant. Notice, that if you constrict the wave, you're increasing the pressure and therefore slightly increasing the density and that would increase the speed. Notice also that as you increase the energy in the wave and the wave gets taller, the bottom of the wave gets faster because of increasing pressure. This is essentially making the speed of the wave more diffused thereby preserving the gaussian function and keeping the bulk speed of the wave constant.

Your venturi at 9:20 only works in a "closed" system like a pipe, or the end of your garden hose when you put your thumb on it. The taper must leave no room for outward expansion for the fluid to accelerate, as it's expansion, at that point, would be, as you said, from right to left. That's why your wave got taller. You have the right deal you were just trying to use a 2D drawing within a 3D system. Also, the Venturi is used to create a vacuum at the point of acceleration. That is how the car wash sprayers with the soap container on the bottom of them work. Your hose water sprays through a Venturi which creates a vacuum that sucks up the soap from the container into the spray nozzle, mixes it, then sprays it on your car.
Pretty sure it's just doing the simple 2D wave equation, not actual fluid dynamics (which would be too computationally intense to do at this resolution and this speed). So wave velocity is constant.

A funnel would increase the velocity of flowing particles of water, assuming constant flow rate through an enclosed pipe (not an open channel like we have here), but it doesn't affect the velocity of a wave propagating through a medium. So the wave should not have sped up at all going through that funnel. And the side wall friction would have slowed particles, reflecting the wave, adding pressure, and increasing the height of the wave, while reducing the flow rate of water towards the end of the funnel. The game correctly modelled all of that.

I actually think the simulation was more accurate than you gave it credit for. The reason you mistakenly thought the water should speed up is because you were thinking of a closed tube system with a constant input of water versus the open three dimensional tunnel system with intermittent waves of water. In a closed tube, water fills up the entire circumference of the tube and therefore has nowhere to expand except going forward along the tube. Therefore if you want the same amount of water to go through a smaller section of tube, it has to move faster. But in your simulation, the water is free to expand upwards as well as forward, so it can get the same amount of water through the narrower passage by basically stacking the water higher. And since the water is coming in waves rather than a constant pressure like in a tube, the water that bounces backwards isnâ€™t being met with the same kind of resistance that it would in the tube scenario.

Just wanted to put this out there as I think you got some hydrology stuff wrong here!  In response to your claim about the water speeding up when the flow path is restricted, this happens because the flow (also describes the energy) of the water wants to remain constant, though the morphology of the terrain can convert the total gravitational potential/kinetic energy of the water back and forth).  Flow is measured in cubic feet or liters per second.  When a river constricts, the entire amount of flowing water is effected, because less water can fit through the space at once it must speed up for the flow to remain constant.  In this experiment you have divided the water up, meaning that the volume of water flowing through these channels will be proportional to the size of the channels and the velocity change will reflect this relationship rather than that of initial speed or total flow to channel size.  I think their water simulation is better than you gave it credit for!  Otherwise very interesting and cool video idea.  -whitewater guide and nerd.

I think that the flaw in this game's water physics is that water travels between map cells based on its speed, which is probably the easiest way to program flow, but as you can see it's not accurate as it is heavily simplified. But that's just a speculation on how it works. If I were to program this (having no engineering degree lol) I would make a powder toy where cells have two values: amount of water and speed vector. Speed vectors of two cells decide how much water is going to be transferred between them. Cells transfer their speed vector to all cells in the 180 cone in the vector direction. Cells adjacent to ground reflect water in all possible directions. Based on the amount of water in a cell it will also try to spread out a small proportional amount in all directions, so waves and ripples gradually die out by themselves (which is obviously not a thing in this game) A lot of tweaking would be needed to make this even look realistic but I think that's the right way to go.

Your assumptions for a tesla valve are assuming that you're in a closed pipe, where the water cannot rise in height, the only variable in a pipe is that the speed can increase or decrease as the aperture decreases or increases respectively.  In your tesla valve, the compression horizontally causes the wave to squeeze in, and because its not a closed pipe, the water volume is instead pushed up vertically to compensate for the reduction in horiontal space, as thats more physically natural for a fluid than an increase in speed (i think gases work the other way around.) Increasing a fluids speed with pressure actually puts quite a high strain on pipes and I believe Barlow's formula is what's used to calculate this pressure for a pipe, even in a transformative section such as a tesla valve, and I imagine is probably the biggest source of strain on a piping material.  So cities skylines is actually simulating fluids reasonably well, as a tldr, its just not a closed system so it might appear counterintuitive. Each "lobe" of the valve should be offset from the next.  Thus, the flow is forced to enter one of the lobes.  The way you have it it is not forced to enter a lobe.  The point at which the lobe feeds back into the main channel is the point at which the next lob branches off on the other side.  So you have a series of V's that feed into U's that lead back into the main channel.
I am sorry to say this design only is intended to work with a steady laminar flow and it is not 100% effective but a very good start for a non powered flow restrictor. Additionally, if you made the inlets to the flow reversor longer than the outlet by a 3:2 ratio with the inlet being more narrow, thereby causing flow to retain more velocity and attack the flow it work work better. Lastly, you must have pressure and fluid dynamics intact, and as such have a top of the system. You can't have that with a water break(ake?). Love your content though. I would suggest a few things:  make the tesla valve along the river to save the inbound/outbound roads and make most of the map usable; make the valve segments shorter than the outer walls to avoid spill over; construct the segments in a tear drop shape to increase the resistance to forward flow; and alternate segments from left to right and reduce that giant gap down the middle.  A primary component of the tesla valve design is that each segment forces a significant amount of the forward flow around the tear drop to create greater resistance to forward flow.
I'm not entirely sure I agree with you that those narrow parts "should affect the speed".. At least not by much; It WOULD be true if it was in a closed pipe, but.. when talking about free-flowing water, it's a matter of what would be the easier option; To speed up/down.. or raise in elevation..  (more water pr second can either be done by speeding up, or by increasing the diameter of the "pipe"  at the same speed, and if it would get higher waves, you will in effect be having a bigger cross-section of water, so it may be transporting the same water..   Also; with the first "wedge" you created a bit of a back-draft, which will slow down the water (The direction it had was straight forward, but sooner or later entropy will make the water notice that there is some.. areas behind it which doesn't have water, and the pressure of the water lower down- from the potential energy of water that is higher up- may mean that instead of staying in the direction, it will actually be a lower potential to turn direction to fill out the gap.. and when you turned the direction of SOME water.. it will be a bit of a suction effect on what is left as well, but either way.. the wave loses energy.. which you could see in the form of a lower wave.   I think the main question is.. how the formulas of fluid-dynamics work out with this type of "force" (well; Momentum).. How "difficult" would it be for the water to actually speed up (remember; There is no engine making SURE that it will keep it's momentum), compared to how much force would be needed to "increase the intersection" by raising the height of the wave. I suspect a tsunami waves move at (well; Close to) the speed that the interaction between water-molecules can handle, at least not within a short distance.. (if you were to prolong the narrow channel all the way to the city I suspect the situation might be a lot different.. IF the physics engine is "good" then it should change the speed, but either way it will stop the back-draft-effect from the hidden compartments behind your wedges)

8:58 in a real-life scenario, you would not see this much water get faster in a situation of THIS scale. a tidal wave that freaking big is already pretty much as fast as its going to get. when waves get this absolutely colossal, it takes *exponentially more* energy to make it go faster and faster, because its just such an absurd amount of mass it's not even funny.  as well, at least from my understanding, the general idea that water gets faster in a situation like this is assuming the water has nowhere else to go, but in this situation the water does have somewhere else to go, more up. in order to get more speed out of it, you have to have this area completely sealed so that the water has nowhere to go but through the funnel, which then pushes more water through it, because there is less water to push back in the opposite direction of the funnel than there is pushing into the funnel, thus faster because less energy is wasted on other movement.

Part of the idea doesn't work. It's supposed to create a bigger pressure drop on a continuous flow. Curving part of the flow backwards is the genius idea. It removes the pressure applied by that part and uses it against the rest of it. A water bender or Tai chi practitioner will appreciate that especially. Here the curving doesn't work. Even if it was shaped nicely, alternating sides and using enough room to curve backwards, it's energy would be directed at the water after the tsunami and the front wave would be unaffected.   What did work is the serrated ( sawtooth ? unsure of the translation) pattern. Basically you stop two thirds of the tsunami by cutting the sides of. The remaining third gets widened again, maybe doubling in width by the time it hits the next split. It doesn't go all the way to sides or you'd have reduced the water to a third again. But halving it a couple times sure is effective enough.   It would be nice to see a very minor pattern on the sides, like a few dozen steps reducing the water by 1/10th or less. Then you'd get a remaining amount of (9/10)^n, which becomes very small fast. With such a wide gap in between it would be way more effective than you guess at first glance.

waves does not move like this. natural ocean waves are not moving only in one direction. they moves in two directions at the same time. one moves forward the other moves backwards. that water is so fake. ocean waves are longitudinal and transverse at the same time. one part diverge the other converge. one goes outward the other part of the wave goes perpendicular. in this simulation the waves moves only in one direction at the time and then collapse eventually when it reach some resistance. real waves does not need any resistance to produce its behavior as its induced by a property of nature that is not based on resistance. those people that made that game does not know how waves really works.

7:01 I can't believe no one is making fun of this shape.
You kept saying that the velocity of the water would be reduced, and while that is actually 100% true in a closed system like a pipe, there is no reason the water would slow down in a situation like this simply because it spread out. There is no pressure differential in this system, being open to the air, like there would be in a pipe. The momentum of the water should therefore carry it as fast as it would go until it is slowed down by the friction and absorption on the ground. It also wouldn't speed up in the narrowing channel so long as it has another direction to spread its mass that isn't forward... in this case up.   So in short, this simulation is even closer to reality than you thought, and most of the things you thought it would do are based on a closed pipe system that isn't limited on the 3rd dimension by only gravity.
I think the way this game simulates this has much more in common with the wave dynamics of light. You should make a diffraction grating or double slit or some other optical thing to see what that does.

ok so just to explain the game's water physics a bit (so you can abuse them better):  The game sees the water height as an X/Y grid with numbers on it. Water moves at a certain speed because that's the speed the devs have limited the math in the background to. Long story short, you can generally ignore speed in this kind of water sim, as it is constant.   What the game cares about however is volume. It knows there's a certain amount of water at an x/y location, and that it has a certain velocity (which is constant in this game as either moving in a direction, or not, in regards to waves). so you have the tidal wave map, lots of water (max volume) all moving in the same direction (all have the same velocity value). what the game does with this is for each 'square' of the grid it sees the map as (subdivided to make the math easier on your computer) it runs a check. "Do i have water" from there it checks "how much" and then check the cells beside it for where that water can go, biased by the direction the water is set to move in.   from there it can move a certain amount of water from each tile to a tile with less water in it. this is the speed limiter the devs have applied.  now when this hits a wall it knows it can't fill "wall" with water so it will raise the water height to a certain limit, based on how much water is behind the front of the wave. since the water can only have one direction out of 360 degrees, the game basically bounces it like a mirror would a laser. if two wave fronts collide, they will add to eachother, or cancel eachother out.  this is very rough in how it works, and i'm sure there's better explainations on youtube, but it should be enough to get the point across.
isn't the tesla valve some measure to allow a *_constant_* stream of water from one side, and cause a *_constant_* stream from the other to "block itself" by being forced to go "backwards" against the direction of the flow ? and that would even be better with a s-waving shape on the inside of that valve. but a straight canal along the middle is open for a single tsunami wave (the "turn back side channels" are just too long to have any effect on the single center wave that already went past), and thus that wave is only reduced by the narrow width and the opportunity for the wave to spread out every time, which has nothing to do with how that type of valve works.  to show how it works, you should build a river with a lot of constant flow (instead of a single tsunami wave) that goes through the valve. depending on the direction, the water should either flow almost unrestricted, and in the other direction it should generate lots of backflow and rotating currents that slow down the water flow and even (because of the general S-shape) force most of the water to go into the next "trap" where the same (backflow, rotation, etc) happens again. and thus in that direction, the water could no longer go through the canal "at full speed" but rise and possibly go over all walls.  as great as many other RCE videos were, and as nice this video is for showing something interesting with waves, as bad is this *_for demonstrating the tesla valve,_* not showing it at all :-( _just search youtube for "tesla valve" to see what it really is._

That's a poor model for a tsunami. You're using a short impulse wave with a very narrow wavelength. A tsunami has a very long wavelength, frequently as much as 500 kilometers (300 miles) or more. As a tsunami approaches the coast, the front of the wave slows down, allowing the rear of the wave to catch up, building the height of the wave as it approaches the shore. It's why a tsunami can be a meter high in the deep ocean, but climb to 10 meters or more near shore.  The valve you use in your model would be overrun as well. As soon as the valve slowed down the front of the wave, the rear of the wave would catch up and spill over the top of the valve.

That's not a Tesla valve. At best it more analogous to a silencer. Which absolutely is not a one way valve.
You keep saying that the water should speed up when the wave hits a narrow part of the channel. This logic is flawed. If you had a _closed pipe_ and no water around your initial "wave" of water, this would be true. In that case, there is a pressure behind the water, forcing it forward. Thus, as the water has nowhere else to go, the only thing the water can do is speed up. The energy needed for the acceleration is "stolen" from the water pressure, and acts a a resistance, slowing the total water flow.  In your case, however, you have a channel (open on top) and a single wall of water travelling through it. When that single wave meets a narrow part of the channel, there is little or no pressure behind it to increase the velocity. There is only the kinetic energy of the water itself. Instead of increasing the speed, the water piles up, as the simulation shows. So even if this is not a professional flow simulating tool, I think that the simulation is fairly accurate.

With open bodies of liquid, games typically do not simulate the bulk movement of the liquid -- instead only simulating the propagation of transverse waves through a fixed medium. This causes the waves to behave much like non-relativistic optical waves.  Similarly, with things like pipes, games typically only simulate the bulk movement of the liquid and do not simulate any kind of wave propagation.

The reason that water flows faster through a narrower pipe is down to conservation of mass: dm/dt remains the same. Since your wave can get taller it doesn't need to go faster to get through the first valve with a constant dm/dt.

Regarding water flowing faster in narrower channels: Bernoulli's principle only applies to incompressible fluids. Your tidal wave is not height constrained, so the wavefront is highly compressible. You would have to fill in a rigid roof on the strongest shape and prevent it from expanding when the fluid gets excited.

I think this engineer misunderstands fluid physics.  The speeding up of fluids as they enter a constricted space happen only as long as the fluid is actually being constricted. With your valve channel the water can still be raised up in level in response to compression.  This means your valve is working the way it should according to real physics while if this was water in a pipe with a tesla valve, it would have no room to be further compressed and thus speed up.  Basicly every part of this replaces increased speed for increased wave height.
I think the ability to move up and down accounts for the energy changes which you are expecting to be in the form of speed. When you are thinking the water should slow down it's actually falling down which turns into extra speed. When you think it should speed up it simply rises instead because the height isn't capped like in a pipe. That being said it is possible that in real life it should be some combination of both results

Yeah... Not a Tesla valve. A series of flat walls at the same points as the tips of your sloped sections will perform ~95% the same due to how water is simulated in this game. A narrow gap into an opening slices the wave in half, then it expands into the new space. Do that ~3 times and the wave is gone just like your "valve" does. If you shape them semicircular with the concave side towards the sea, then you will still get the neato deflections. The "narrow pipe flows faster" principle depends on it being a closed, full pipe. The speed increases because flow rate has to remain the same throughout the length of the pipe, so the change in cross sectional area results in an opposite change in speed because flow rate = speed x area. But the tsunami in your scenario has room to conserve flow rate by changing it's height - basically, the cross sectional area is allowed to remain constant.

The first "funnel", as you put it, that you built was not accurate. It would speed up as it went through the funnel, but at the opening the volume increase. This means you built an Orifice. In engineering school you should have learned (but probably forgotten unless you use it frequently) is that you created an expansion device. Restrict the flow, then allow it to go back to the same volume as before means the pressure decreases and speed decreases between the start of the orifice and the end. (in the middle of the orifice the speed will increase)

Aren't the Venturi tunnels meant to work only with gases? Gases can be compressed or can expand, unlike liquids which can't compress or expand. In my thermodynamics classes we study that for jet engines and a nuzzle at the exit of the engine will affect the speed of the exhaust gases and the pressure of those gases too. If you can't change the pressure of the fluid then you can't change its velocity too otherwise you're messing with Bernoulli's principle (at least that's what I think I remember lol). Fluid dynamics studies both gases and liquids which kinda bothers me because although some things work for both, other stuff, like the Venturi effect, works only with gases.   Otherwise great vid! I'm not sure the Tesla valve works the same way with open channel flow vs pipe flow. The speed of the wave doesn't slow down because the height (pressure head) goes down instead of the velocity head. Though realistically it is working to slow down the wave, but not sure more effectively than just sequeezing down the width of the channel and putting in a disturbance to act as an energy loss. Essentially you are back to the isolated wave breaks like you'd have to reduce energy at the end of a spillway.

9:27 one thing you are confused about here is wave propagation is not a steady state condition such as water continuously flowing under pressure which would increase as the pressure dropped and width of the channel reduced. However, steady state conditions are not the situation here as waves behave differently then steady state flow and cause reflections which is what you are seeing. the model in this game actually look very similar to real physics which is understandable as waves are not a very difficult thing to simulate. The propagation speed of the wave would certainly not change as that is an inherent parameter based on other factors such as viscosity or pressure which are constant in this simulation. what you actually created here is an impedance mismatch so some of the wave is reflected backwards toward the source with the remainder continuing which is why it was smaller coming out of that channel and had a similar effect regardless of which way the walls were sloped. You can also clearly see all the reflections in the smaller waves that are all spread out instead of being concentrated in one large wave which is why the height was so reduced so actually this method of creating just a bunch of funnels in series would still reduce the energy in the wave propagating through as they would reflect energy back toward the source at each stage.  and again at 13:36 that is very realistic since that center part of the wave remained unobstructed through the entire channel so why would you expect it to slow down? Again the speed of the wave remains constant but the energy is being reduced since must of it is reflected back toward the source.  you could retry this experiment with steady state condition i.e. a river but I suspect it still will not work because this is not a tesla valve since tesla valves are fully enclosed so pressure can actually work. Instead what would likely happen is since the flow rate of the river would be constant instead of the pressure being constant then it would just force itself over the walls as it is not enclosed, but it would be interesting to see what happens anyways.

So a Tesla valve is designed to work in a system where you have water flowing through a pipe, and you want the water to flow one way but not the other. In a case like that, the water is likely being moved by a pump, which creates pressure (or said differently, adds energy) to push the water in the desired direction. In the case of a wave, the mechanics are completely different. Here, most of the energy is actually used to move the water particles up and down. The illusion of the water "pushing" toward the shore is the same as watching a crowd of people "do the wave" in a stadium; no one is actually moving from their seat, but you still see the crest of the wave travel. The game doesn't seem to recognize that the tsunami will dissipate once it hits shore and literally runs out of water to make waves with, but it does seem reasonably accurate with how the waves reflect, diffract, and lose energy as they hit a barrier.

Looking at the two angled walls, getting rid of the water, I think I figured out how the water physics work that are so wonky; there isn't actual pressure on the wave from the water behind it, pushing it forward. IRL, the whole of the water behind the wave would still be impacting it, but there doesn't seem to be any kind of "weight" to the water, so when it hits the 2 angled walls, it pushes that water at a very acute angle, simulating the physics, without the water behind continuing to push it. I don't think I am explaining this well, but essentially, the water that doesn't get forced forward instead goes back towards the exit. Water seems to be able to flow back THROUGH the water flowing forward.

Two problems with this simulation: (as you say at the end of the video) 1. A tesla valve works against a constant flow of water. This wave is a single narrow surge or wall of "water" (yes, with a secondary resonance wave behind it) which leaves a shallow film of "water" behind it that immediately starts moving in the opposite direction (receding). In order for flowing water to speed up as it gets constricted it has to have water pushing it from behind, which is not the case here. 2. (Why I put "water" in quotes) This is not a volumetric fluid simulation with all the dynamics of actual fluid. It's a particle simulation. Each particle has a local warping or "vertex displacement" effect on a 3D mesh "skin" representing the surface of the water shared with other particles. The particles are assigned physical properties like *friction, mass,* and *elasticity* to be effected by environment gravity and response to collisions with other objects. Particle *attraction* equates to fluid viscosity - how faithfully one particle "sticks" to another. Generally, this is good enough to simulate water flowing constantly in a river or through a structure like a sluice, but in truth it's more like low friction marbles rolling down a trough with a thin veil of elastic film covering it.  Where the particle simulation misses the mark in fluid dynamics is in simulating wave physics. In particle physics, each particle supports the mass of the particle on top of it as long as it has a solid surface below it. This means the initial, full volume of the wave can travel endlessly across a flat plane until it collides with an obstruction or the tiny amount of friction eventually erodes the bottom of the mound of particles. In fluid dynamics and wave physics, the full volume and mass of the wave is supported by an equal volume of fluid beneath it. It takes at least 10 feet of water to support a ten foot wave making a total of 20 feet. As soon as a 10 foot wave runs into (say) a 5 foot depth, the wave will break over it's top and reduce down to a 5 foot wave. If this was real the initial wave should have crested and broken down to churning foam as soon as it hit the shallow depth of the channel. Your set up is simply taking successive bites out of the total wave volume or total number of particles moving forward. A true volumetric fluid simulator takes a mega computer to calculate in real time.

9:45 The formula A1 * v1 = A2 * v2 (for an incompressible fluid like water we can ignore density) works only in one directional motion. When the canal narrowed, if the only direction that the water can go is left, then yes your velocity would have increased. However, the water was able to move upwards, meaning your cross section area of the water flow pretty much didnâ€™t change, which is why you see no change in velocity

The way games usually simulate water is by generating a mesh of semi-random vertices, then connecting those vertices with edges that work like springs (they literally use the same formula). Some post processing is then done to make the surface look better and smoother but, under the hood, it's still a cloud of floaty, springy points holding hands.  You could try out Blender's fluid physics to check how much more accurate they are. Staring at Blender's grey shapes is probably not that fun, but you could probably get someone to build a cute playground.

The fluid mechanics in this game are very simple. the fluid will not move faster or slower, it just keeps moving at a constant speed and bounces off obstacles. Therefore, the best defense against a tsunami is simply a few horizontal lines, each subtracting its height from the height of the tsunami. and we need to add some small staggered channels so that the water can drain back into the ocean.

Are you absolutely sure the water physics scale up the way you think? Most valves etc are designed for pipes,  which are significantly smaller scale than a tsunami, and where things like the hydrogen bonds of water have some small impact.  Essentially increasing scale would effectly cause the fluid to have less surface tension and change the domain of things like Reynolds number etc.  Ocean sized mechanics probably rely far more on wave dynamics.  With that in mind,  you could have actually ran in parallel with multiple different valve designs simultaneously to reduce render time.    Also the final design of the Tesla valve also has slightly  overlapping and alternating lobes or valves.

A further adaptation would be this: instead of having a big wall all the way across the bay with just one Tesla valve channel, build a set of Tesla channels, all the way across the width of the bay. Love it! The Tesla valve is so simple but effective, and has no moving parts to maintain. I think it's actually used today in a few applications. I do feel you could make offset parallel valve streams, though, to cover the width of  the land area.  I know this was probably mostly for fun, but I'm interested in the practice of the valve in such a scenario. So...  One good defense approach against a wave/tsunami is a concave wall. The Tesla valve is pretty much a series of offset, mirrored concave walls. I wonder: if we just take the one side of the valve, flip it so that the back-flow is facing down to the ground, and build it leading into the ground as a series of back-flow funnels (no top/roofing), how effective that'd be. It'd take up a long stretch of land to do, but you'd essentially be chipping away at the underneath of the wave at each back-flow point. This way, you wouldn't need to build up, you'd just need to dig down. 19:00 there is a lot of free, open source software which is used in engineering, physics research etc., that simulates fluid dynamics correctly, and is used in real science. The on that's used most in engineering afaik, is OpenFoam.
Isnâ€˜t it much more of a difference in speed, if you have a pipe with fixed area compared to a open canal, where the Area depends of the hight of the water?  Because in a pipe, the formula Q=v*A with fixed Q means the velocity is directly antiproportional to the area. In an open rectangular canal, the formula Q=v*A can be written as Q=v*w*h, so when changing the with of the canal, the water gets deeper or shallower, but the velocity changes only little
I think the physics are actually correct, what you expect applies for sealed pipes, where a reduction in area increases the speed and reduces the pressure. In this case most of the water is at atmospheric pressure-ish, so we won't see the effect of dynamic forces. What we saw in the area reduction example was an increase in the head (height) of the wave, which can occur as there are no volume constraints.

While the design idea is right, that's not quite the proper design for a Tesla valve. There should be no straight through-and-through channel down the middle. It should have a slight winding curve that won't allow water to pass straight through. This is made by having the diverging channels staggered against each other on both sides and having the walls extend past the center point. Essentially, each turn of the winding center channel is forcing fluid down these diverging side channels to create a blackflow and turbulence. It's this created backflow and turbulence that makes a Tesla valve function as one-way valve. Theoretically, a proper designed Tesla valve should stop the constant pressure of an elevated ocean, not just a single tsunami wave, and thus make a city below sea level possible.

RCE, you have misunderstood the idea of Tesla valve: it should not work with a tsunami wave at all! The real idea is that the reversed fluxes hit the main flux "head to head" and thus stop it (or, at least, greatly increase the hydrodynamic resistance of the valve). And note that this implies having a rather prolonged push of water! But when you're dealing with a tsunami wave, by the time the reversed fluxes could be pushed back into the main channel, the "main flux" exists no more there: the "direct main wave" has already gone forward and there's no water to push back.  Tesla valves work when they are initially fully filled with a liquid/gas and there is a PRESSURE wave incoming, not MASS wave into an empty system!

I'm not sure if the wavefront would actually slow down IRL, because the wave is not like a 2D-fluid or something. It can get bigger or lower which is not possible for 2D fluids (or confided 3d fluids for that matter). So I think that reducing the height of the wave is actually the more realistic way of reducing the energy of the wave. I wouldn't really expect the group velocity to change that much in this context. Perhaps in other words: As far as I understand, the tesla valve is primarily designed to stop a constant laminal flow. A tsunami however is a singular wave-packet, which is also not really confined to the tesla valve like a 2D fluid would be. So it still makes sense for the valve to reduce the wave packets energy, but for me it doesn't make intuitive sense to change the dispersion of the water waves and thereby change the velocity of the packet. (I would never expect a wave of lower energy to be slower, I would always expect it to have a lower amplitude.)

A couple of points to note: Tesla valves aren't straight, they kink as they go along to force flow into one of the traps - you could model this by making one of the arms twice the length of the other and then alternating them; Also, Tesla valves are _one-way_ - should be perfectly feasible for the river to drain through the Tesla valve even while it stops the tsunami.
